        function gptClean(gptText) {

                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');

            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))

    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="5a2fe73195184cd1b8193e2c7177ac7b"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="5a2fe73195184cd1b8193e2c7177ac7b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'df811977-3fcc-46ee-8ffd-01d6055a1d27'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn figures-tab cloned-with-ids empty"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-multi-figure"></i><h2>Figures</h2></a></li><li role="presentation" class="tab__nav__item"><a id="pane-pcw-referencescon-cloned" href="#pane-pcw-references" data-slide-clone="self" aria-controls="pane-pcw-references" role="menuitem" data-slide-target="#pane-pcw-references" class="tab__nav__item__link article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn references-tab cloned-with-ids empty"><i aria-hidden="true" aria-controls="pane-pcw-related" role="menuitem" data-slide-target="#pane-pcw-related" class="tab__nav__item__link article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn related-tab cloned-with-ids empty"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-related-circles-extend-button"></i><h2>Related</h2></a></li><li role="presentation" class="tab__nav__item"><a id="pane-pcw-detailscon-cloned" href="#pane-pcw-details"         <div class="relative"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" role="button" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" aria-label="Tools" class="article-tools__ctrl"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Tools"></i><span>Tools</span></a><div data-db-target-of="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" aria-labelledby="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" class="article-tools__block fixed dropBlock__holder"><ul role="menu" class="rlist w-slide--list"><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/action/rightsLink?doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630&amp;mode=" role="menuitem" target="_blank"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-permissions-key"></i><span>Request permission</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-export-icon"></i><span>Export citation</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/personalize/addFavoritePublication?doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-favorites"></i><span>Add to favorites</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/addCitationAlert?doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Track-citations"></i><span>Track citation</span></a></li></ul></div></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d23124626" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion hybrid capacitors (SIHCs) have attracted much attention due to integrating the high energy density of battery and high out power of supercapacitors. However, rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in cathode is counterbalanced with sluggish anode, hindering the further advancement and commercialization of SIHCs. Here, aiming at conversion-type metal sulfide anode, taking typical VS<sub>2</sub> as an example, a comprehensive regulation of nanostructure and electronic properties through NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalation and Mo-doping VS<sub>2</sub> (Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>) is reported. It is demonstrated that NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalation can enlarge the interplanar spacing and Mo-doping can induce interlayer defects and sulfur vacancies that are favorable to construct new ion transport channels, thus resulting in significantly enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and pseudocapacitance. Density functional theory calculations further reveal that the introduction of NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> and Mo-doping enhances the electronic conductivity, lowers the diffusion energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup>, and produces stronger d-p hybridization to promote conversion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation intermediates. Consequently, Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> delivers a record-high reversible capacity of 453 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> and an ultra-stable cycle life of over 20&nbsp;000 cycles. The assembled SIHCs achieve impressive energy density/power density of 98&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>/11.84&nbsp;kW kg<sup>−1</sup>, ultralong cycling life of over 15000 cycles, and very low self-discharge rate (0.84&nbsp;mV h<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202308630-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202308630-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Among various energy storage technologies, electrochemical storage devices have received extensive attention as important energy storage means of facilitating grid synchronization of power generated by using regenerable energies and development of automobile electrification.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0001_R_d23124618e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, lithium-ion battery is the largest share of electrochemical storage technology due to its advantages of high energy density and long cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0002_R_d23124618e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy Carbon">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, due to the rapid increase in demand for portable electronic products, electric vehicles and other renewable energy products, energy storage devices that meet both high energy density and high-power density are urgently needed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0003_R_d23124618e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun. Chem. Eng. J.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Non-aqueous hybrid ion capacitors are a new class of electrochemical energy storage devices with energy density between batteries and supercapacitors, and at the same time can display comparable power density and long cycle life with supercapacitors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0004_R_d23124618e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Since the first Li-ion hybrid capacitor based on nanostructured Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> (anode) and activated carbon (cathode) was reported in 2001, the hybrid ion capacitor has been rapidly advanced and commercialized.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0005_R_d23124618e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ChemElectroChem Adv. Sustain. Syst.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, due to the limitation of global lithium resource maldistribution and fluctuant lithium price, which cannot meet the growing demand in the field of large-scale energy storage. Therefore, sodium-based energy storage devices have emerged as alternatives. Sodium-ion hybrid capacitors (SIHCs) are attracting attention and showing great potential, combining sodium-ion battery materials as anodes, capacitor electrode materials as cathodes, and organic sodium-ion battery electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0006_R_d23124618e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In 2012, the concept of SIHC was first proposed and validated by Kuratani et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0007_R_d23124618e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Electrochim. Acta">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They used hard carbon as the battery-type anode, activated carbon as the capacitive-type cathode, and 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (EC/DEC, vol = 1/1) as the electrolyte. This hybrid ion capacitor had 91% capacity retention after 1000 cycles, thus demonstrating the great potential for developing SIHCs. However, Na<sup>+</sup> has a larger radius (1.02 Å) compared to Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), resulting in sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction kinetics during charge/discharge process, thus lacking high-capacity anodes with fast reaction kinetics to match the capacitive cathodes, and the resulting kinetic imbalance greatly limits the development of high-performance SIHCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0008_R_d23124618e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So in this regard, there is an urgent need to develop anode materials with fast electrochemical reaction kinetics, high-rate performance, low cost and good cycling stability to match with capacitive cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0009_R_d23124618e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In recent years, transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) have been widely developed for energy storage materials because of their high theoretical specific capacities and graphite-like open two-dimensional layered structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0010_R_d23124618e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nanoscale">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, previous researches indicate that most of TMDs suffer from continuous phase conversion during sodiation/de-sodiation process, resulting in serious volume change and structure pulverization. In addition, due to the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics in bulk TMDs also exhibits poor rate performance and cyclic stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0011_R_d23124618e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Batteries Supercaps Adv. Fiber Mater. Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To address these issues, researchers have proposed strategy to change the layered structure to improve Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics such as pre-intercalation Li<sup>+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0012_R_d23124618e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Lett.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>、Na<sup>+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0013_R_d23124618e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>、K<sup>+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0014_R_d23124618e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>、Mg<sup>2+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0015_R_d23124618e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>、NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0016_R_d23124618e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and organic molecule<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0017_R_d23124618e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. to expand the layer spacing of (001) plane. It shows that the pre-intercalated ions or molecules can act as a pillar support to enhance ion transport within (00l) plane. The extended layer spacing can also alleviate the volume expansion to some extent during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0018_R_d23124618e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Ecomat">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, pre-intercalation TMDs can have the ability to achieve superior cycling stability. In addition, recent studies prove that heteroatom doping is an effective method for regulating electronic structure, which change the energy band structure to improve the conductivity and accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0019_R_d23124618e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nanotechnology">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cation doping in TMDs may also introduce sulfur vacancies, thereby adding more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0020_R_d23124618e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Inorg. Chem. Front. J. Colloid Interface Sci.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Doped TMDs can exhibit increased reversible specific capacity and rate performance.</p>
         
         <p>These two strategies improve the electrochemical performance of TMDs in terms of material nanostructure or electronic structure modulation, respectively. Combining these two strategies may synergistically enhance the stability of structure as well as boost electrochemical reactive kinetics to achieve both high specific capacity and long cycling stability of TMDs. Regulating material nanostructure can effectively suppress the agglomeration of TMDs and increase more active surfaces and edges. These exposed surfaces and edges can provide more active sites for the intra-layer pseudocapacitive storage of Na<sup>+</sup>. Engineering electronic structure of TMDs may ameliorate insufficient charge transport capability due to the low intrinsic conductivity of TMDs. Combing unrestricted Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels by expanding interplanar spacing would surely enhance Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The synergistic enhancement is expected to mitigate insufficient electrochemical kinetics for TMDs anodes in SIHCs.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above considerations, this study chose vanadium disulfide (VS<sub>2</sub>) as a model material in TMDs. Compared to other TMDs (e.g., MoS<sub>2</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, WS<sub>2</sub>), VS<sub>2</sub> has stable 1T phase structure, metallic-like properties, low Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier, and high theoretical capacity (932 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) based on the complete conversion reaction from VS<sub>2</sub> to V<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S due to its multi-electron conversion capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0021_R_d23124618e684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, VS<sub>2</sub> suffers from similar drawbacks as other TMDs that is prone to stacking grow in two-dimensional orientation during synthesis, which would greatly reduce the number of active sites and limit Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0022_R_d23124618e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A Infomat">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose a novel strategy for synergistic regulation of nanostructures and electronic properties by NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalation and Mo doping. The intercalated NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> imparts significantly enlarged interlayer spacing, accelerate ion diffusion, and stabilizes the layered structure. The double defects introduced by Mo doping not only furnish Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites, but also enhance transfer kinetics by providing rapid cross-layer migration pathways. The synergistic effect of nanostructure regulation and electronic structure adjustment endows VS<sub>2</sub> with enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and capacitive storage capacity, demonstrating rate performance of up to 453 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as well as long cycling life over 20&nbsp;000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Benefiting from the excellent performance of the anode, the assembled SIHC exhibits satisfactory power density (11.84&nbsp;kW kg<sup>−1</sup>), cycle life (energy retention of 85% for 15&nbsp;000 cycles), and self-discharge rate (0.84&nbsp;mV h<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202308630-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202308630-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussions</h2>
         
         <p>As shown in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#smll202308630-fig-0008">1</a>, VS<sub>2</sub> with NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalation and Mo-doping synergistically modulated was synthesized by a simple one-step solvothermal method. The (001) lattice plane of VS<sub>2</sub> exhibits the lowest surface energy and the lower interlayer van der Waals forces. As a result, NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> easily enters the interlayers during the reaction process, leading to the expansion of the interlayer distance. It is worth noting that Mo<sup>4+</sup> replaces part of V<sup>4+</sup> in the VS<sub>2</sub> lattice, resulting in a large number of sulfur vacancies and unsaturated edge sulfur (called interlayer defects). The construction of double defect can provide abundant Na<sup>+</sup> storage active sites and facilitates charge transfer within the layer. In addition, it can also provides Na<sup>+</sup> cross-layer migration channels to significantly enhances Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Besides, Mo doping modulated the morphology of VS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets to form three-dimensional flower-like spheres. It further promotes the penetration of electrolyte and improves the utilization of active material, and also reduces the internal stress caused by the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202308630-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/728a7f97-fc4d-43d8-b470-15dff26bd6fd/smll202308630-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/728a7f97-fc4d-43d8-b470-15dff26bd6fd/smll202308630-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2691d844-450d-46db-84e9-dbc3491c91b4/smll202308630-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/728a7f97-fc4d-43d8-b470-15dff26bd6fd/smll202308630-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308630-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the microstructural modulation of 5% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> by Mo doping.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The corresponding morphologies and microstructure characterizations of the prepared samples are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1a,e</a> show the SEM images of 3%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> with different magnifications. The sample can be observed to consist of curved nanosheets cross-linked with each other and assembled into 3D structure. By contrast, NVS<sub>2</sub> is nanosheet structure with the size of ≈200–300&nbsp;nm. These nanosheets are closely stacked and agglomerated with each other. The morphology and agglomeration of annealed VS<sub>2</sub> still did not change (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). This may be because Mo<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup> species in solution can react with S<sup>2−</sup> species to generate 2D cores, and then Mo and V species in solution can continuously interact with primary 2D cores from different directions, which further form nanosheets crossing along different directions and forming flower-like morphology during solvothermal process. When the doping amount is 5% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1b,f</a>), the sample further assembled to 3D flower-like structure from many thin nanosheets with smaller nanosheets size compare with 3% doped sample. It is likely that the high molybdenum content promotes initial nucleation and may also favors diffusion controlled subsequent crystal growth. However, when the doping amount increase to 10% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1c,g</a>), the original 3D flower-like structure is destroyed and the nanosheets size gets larger and begin to stack again. But compared to the NVS<sub>2</sub> sample, the nanosheets are not very agglomerated with each other before. When further increasing the amount of doping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1d,h</a>), this stacking situation further intensifies and eventually presents a “crepe cake” shape. The specific surface area and pore size distribution of samples were further characterized by nitrogen adsorption-desorption isotherms (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). All samples show type IV isotherms with H4 hysteresis loops, indicating that the samples have a mesoporous structure. The specific surface areas of NVS<sub>2</sub>, 3%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, 10%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> and 20%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> according to BET test results are 13.8, 59.7, 60.1, 57.4, and 25.4 m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with NVS<sub>2</sub>, Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> samples have mesoporous and macroporous distribution. The larger specific surface area and pore volume not only facilitate electrolyte penetration, but also promote ion transfer with buffer volume changes, thus well improving the electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202308630-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/16806928-c93c-4dd1-829e-e95e8ad3eb3d/smll202308630-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/16806928-c93c-4dd1-829e-e95e8ad3eb3d/smll202308630-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/17ec271a-ea8a-4555-9c42-3002e0c6289f/smll202308630-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/16806928-c93c-4dd1-829e-e95e8ad3eb3d/smll202308630-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308630-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of the a,e) 3%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, b,f) 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, c,g) 10%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, and d,h) 20%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. i) Low-magnification TEM image, j) high-magnification TEM images, k) SAED pattern, l) elemental mapping images of the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>; the inset in Figure (j) shows a profile plot of the calibration for measuring the spacings.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The TEM of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> proved that it was assembled from many inter-cross-linked nanosheets into a flower-like structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1i</a>). It was observed that the 0.96&nbsp;nm d-spacing from the VS<sub>2</sub> extended (001) plane (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1j</a>). Meanwhile, a lot of discontinuous lattice fringe can be observed along the lamellar dimension, which are considered as one-dimensional (1D) interlayer defect resulting from the unsaturated edge sulfur by Mo<sup>4+</sup> replaces part of V<sup>4+</sup> in the VS<sub>2</sub> lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0032_R_d23124618e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1j</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1k</a>) shows two concentric bright diffraction rings, attributing to the (100) and (110) crystal planes of VS<sub>2</sub> without other impurity. The EDS mapping image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0001">1l</a>) further verifies the uniform distribution of Mo, V, S and N elements. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) shows the elemental content of each sample tested by EDS and the concentration of doped Mo elements in each sample from ICP-OES analysis. The Mo element content is somewhat different from the theoretical analytical value, which indicates that only part of the Mo<sup>4+</sup> can be successfully doped into the lattice of VS<sub>2</sub> during the reaction process.</p>
         
         <p>The XRD of the Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> samples are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2a</a>. These main diffraction peaks at 32°, 35.7°, 57.1°, and 58.3° shows similar diffraction peaks with VS<sub>2</sub> (PDF#89-1640). The (001) crystal plane located at 15.4° shifted to 9.6°. This is because small molecules/ions can be embedded between VS<sub>2</sub> layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0023_R_d23124618e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The interplanar spacing can be calculated to be 0.92&nbsp;nm, and the increase spacing is consistent with the size of the NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> (≈3.5 Å).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0018_R_d23124618e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Ecomat">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> No other peaks are observed after the doping with Mo elements, while (001) crystalline planes of four Mo doped VS<sub>2</sub> are shifted to lower angle, more shift with increasing Mo content. It means the introduction of Mo<sup>4+</sup> with larger radius (139 pm) into the VS<sub>2</sub> crystal structure may replaces V<sup>4+</sup> with smaller radius (136 pm), resulting in further layer spacing expansion of NVS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0024_R_d23124618e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Electrochim. Acta">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the peak intensity of the diffraction peak at the (001) crystal plane for Mo doped VS<sub>2</sub> increases with the increase of doping concentration. It implies that increase of doping concentration makes the original VS<sub>2</sub> more inclined to stack growth in the z-axis direction. This result is consistent with the SEM images.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202308630-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ff8abc5d-9ece-401e-9db0-b68ffd1dc45d/smll202308630-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ff8abc5d-9ece-401e-9db0-b68ffd1dc45d/smll202308630-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6a4afe29-f286-4f0f-b097-1ac0f113b6a8/smll202308630-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ff8abc5d-9ece-401e-9db0-b68ffd1dc45d/smll202308630-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308630-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of the NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub>; the left in Figure (a) is the magnified region of 2θ = 7.0 – 11.0°. b) FTIR spectra, c) DSC thermal spectra of the NVS<sub>2</sub> and annealed VS<sub>2</sub>. d) Raman spectra of the NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub>. e) XPS high-resolution spectra of N 1s for the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, NVS<sub>2</sub> and annealed VS<sub>2</sub>. XPS high-resolution spectra of f) Mo 3d, g) V 2p, h) S 2p for the NVS<sub>2</sub>, and 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. i) EPR spectra of the NVS<sub>2</sub> and 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further confirm the intercalation of NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>, FTIR and DSC characterization was performed. There is a strong bending vibrations in the ≈1627 and ≈1387 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to N-H groups in NVS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0018_R_d23124618e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Ecomat">18</a>, <a href="#smll202308630-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0026_R_d23124618e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, it can be observed that the FTIR spectra of both samples show significant absorption peaks at ≈620 cm<sup>−1</sup> and ≈950 cm<sup>−1</sup>, ascribing to the typical S-V-S bond and the stretching of the terminal S (S = V) in VS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0027_R_d23124618e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - New J. Chem.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The DSC curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2c</a>) shows a clear peak of heat absorption at ≈240°C for the NVS<sub>2</sub> sample, indicating that the intercalated NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> decomposition temperature is higher than 240°C .<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0026_R_d23124618e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a>, <a href="#smll202308630-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0028_R_d23124618e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> is able to stabilize in the interlayer without decomposition under normal conditions. The above research shows that the insertion of NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> into the inner structure of VS<sub>2</sub> enlarges the (001) interlayer spacing of VS<sub>2</sub>. In addition, the intercalated NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> has stable column-supporting properties, which predicts its stabilizing role in the subsequent electrochemical reaction.</p>
         
         <p>In order to clarify the NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalated VS<sub>2</sub>, these samples with Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub> and NVS<sub>2</sub> were annealed at 400°C. It is observed that (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) all diffraction peaks of samples after annealing matched well with the diffraction peaks of layered hexagonal VS<sub>2</sub> (PDF#89-1640), which means NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> is detached from the VS<sub>2</sub> crystal structure. Meanwhile, a gradual shift of the diffraction peak of the (001) crystal plane representing VS<sub>2</sub> to a lower angle is also observed with increasing Mo<sup>4+</sup> doping concentration.</p>
         
         <p>The Raman spectra of NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub> display two characteristic peaks at ≈284 and ≈406 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which are assigned to the in-plane E<sub>1g</sub> (opposite vibrations of the two S atoms with respect to the V atoms) and out-of-plane A<sub>1g</sub> (symmetric displacement and compression force of the S atoms along the c axis) vibrational modes of VS<sub>2</sub>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0025_R_d23124618e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater. Inorg. Chem. Front. Chem. Eng. J.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, two peaks at ≈141 and ≈993 cm<sup>−1</sup> are caused by the stretching and bending vibrations of Mo-S and V-S. The intensity of these two peaks decreases with the increase of Mo<sup>4+</sup> doping concentration, indicating the existence of more Mo-S bonds in VS<sub>2</sub> crystal, revealing the increase of interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0025_R_d23124618e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater. Inorg. Chem. Front. Chem. Eng. J.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS for the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, NVS<sub>2</sub>, and annealed VS<sub>2</sub> are also detected. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2e</a> reveals there is a peak observed at 400.9&nbsp;eV for NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, ascribing to the N-H binding energy in NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> complexes in both NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0028_R_d23124618e1172" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">28</a>, <a href="#smll202308630-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0029_R_d23124618e1172" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The two additional Mo 3p peaks in 5% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> compared to NVS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) confirms the presence of Mo elements. 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> displays two peaks at 231.4 and 228.2&nbsp;eV, which are attributed to Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> in the Mo<sup>4+</sup> valence state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2f</a>). Two weak peaks at 233.2&nbsp;eV and 229.5&nbsp;eV indexed to the Mo3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Mo3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Mo<sup>5+</sup>, confirm the presence of Mo<sup>4+</sup> and Mo<sup>5+</sup> species after Mo doping. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2g</a> shows the high-resolution XPS spectrum of V 2p, two strong peaks in NVS<sub>2</sub> located at 523.3&nbsp;eV and 515.9&nbsp;eV correspond to the V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> of V<sup>4+</sup>. Those strong peaks in 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> correspond to V<sup>4+</sup> (523.6&nbsp;eV, 516.0&nbsp;eV) and V<sup>2+</sup> (520.9&nbsp;eV, 512.9&nbsp;eV), respectively. The presence of V<sup>2+</sup> is responsible for the existence of unsaturated sulfur at the crystal structure edge of VS<sub>2</sub> as well as the presence of sulfur vacancies inside the crystals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0030_R_d23124618e1230" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Eng. J. Materials Today Nano">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is noteworthy that these peaks representing V<sup>4+</sup> in the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> sample are shifted towards high binding energy. This is due to the introduction of greater electronegativity Mo elements than that of V, resulting in a lower electron cloud density around V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0031_R_d23124618e1240" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the S 2p spectrum of NVS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2h</a>), the major double peaks at 160.8&nbsp;eV and 162.0&nbsp;eV are attributed to lattice sulfur (S<sup>2−</sup>), which is from the in-plane S<sup>2−</sup> species of VS<sub>2</sub>. Compared to NVS<sub>2</sub>, 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> showed two additional peaks at 163.1 and 164.3&nbsp;eV. Those are attributed to the bridging disulfide ligand (S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup>) in VS<sub>2</sub>. The presence of bridging disulfide S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> ligand are widely considered to be an unsaturated sulfur at the exposed edge of TMDs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0032_R_d23124618e1270" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of more unsaturated edge sulfur forms so-called “interlayer defects”. In addition, the S<sup>2−</sup> binding energy representing the V-S bond in 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> is positively shifted by ≈0.2&nbsp;eV compared to NVS<sub>2</sub>, due to the presence of sulfur vacancies. The transfer of electrons from sulfide to vacancies would reduce the equilibrium electron cloud density, endowing the enhancement of the binding energies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0033_R_d23124618e1282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Electrochim. Acta J. Colloid Interface Sci.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> EPR tests were performed to further confirm the introduction of sulfur vacancies after Mo<sup>4+</sup> doping. Compared with NVS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0002">2i</a>), 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> shows a strong signal at <i>g</i> = 2.0037, which is caused by the electrons trapped of the sulfur vacancies in the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0034_R_d23124618e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Mater. Chem. A">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the CV curve of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> during initial five cycles. Two reduction peaks at 2.18 and 1.56&nbsp;V in the first cathodic scanning present the stepwise Na<sup>+</sup> embedding process of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. A peak at 1.03&nbsp;V is owing to the ubsequent conversion reactions. Further reduction peaks at 0.68 and 0.55&nbsp;V are thought to be the result of complex phase transitions arising from multi-step Na<sup>+</sup> insertion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0025_R_d23124618e1320" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater. Inorg. Chem. Front. Chem. Eng. J.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, there is a sharp and strong peak below 0.5&nbsp;V, which is attributed to the complete transformation of the intermediate phase product.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0021_R_d23124618e1325" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">21</a>, <a href="#smll202308630-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0035_R_d23124618e1325" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Alloys Compd.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the subsequent anodic process, the multiple anodic peaks are derived from the reversible conversion reaction and Na<sup>+</sup> de-intercalation from the material to reform Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. There is a good overlap of CV curves in the subsequent cycles, indicating that the insertion/extraction reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> was stable for 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the second CV cycles of NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub>. It can be seen that 5% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> showed the highest intensity redox peak at 1.94, 1.48, 1.56, and 2.14&nbsp;V and largest CV area, which indicates that the electrode has higher electrochemical activity. NVS<sub>2</sub> has a higher specific capacity than that of annealed VS<sub>2</sub>. It displays a specific capacity of 176 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3A g<sup>−1</sup>, while the annealed VS<sub>2</sub> electrode only has 65 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information). It is noteworthy that the initial coulombic efficiency of the NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode is 83.1%, lower compared to that of the annealed VS<sub>2</sub> (86.3%). However, the NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode has a higher platforms capacity, resulting of the extended layer spacing by NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalation layers (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the extended layer spacing facilitates rapid ion diffusion and maintains structural integrity during repeated cycling. As expected, the NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode has superior cycling stability, with no significant capacity degradation over 500 cycles. In contrast, anealed VS<sub>2</sub> showed severe capacity decay after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202308630-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e0d09da3-cde3-4116-8db2-e3f204e9847e/smll202308630-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e0d09da3-cde3-4116-8db2-e3f204e9847e/smll202308630-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a2a33c2c-8604-4dea-8995-742351e2388b/smll202308630-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e0d09da3-cde3-4116-8db2-e3f204e9847e/smll202308630-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308630-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub>: a) CV curves of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> with initial five cycles at 0.5 mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) CV curves at 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> for the second cycle, c) rate performance and d) long-term cycling performance at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of the NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub>. e) Discharge-charge curves of the different cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> of the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. f) The sodium storage performance of this work is compared with other work. g) Long-term cycling performance at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of the NVS<sub>2</sub> and 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Meanwhile, Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub> shows better rate performance at all current densities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0003">3c</a>), which 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> delivers specific capacities of 602, 554, 539, 527, 504, and 453 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.5, 1.0, and 3.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0003">3d</a> shows the cycling performance of NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub>. The reversible capacity of the electrode is further enhanced by doping with Mo elements. 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode has a reversible specific capacity of 497 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention rate of 98%. The CE remains almost at 100% after capacity stabilization, indicating that the electrochemical reactions of Na<sup>+</sup> are highly reversible. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0003">3e</a> shows the charge/discharge at a constant current for the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode of 1A g<sup>−1</sup> at different cycles. It exhibits high initial discharge and charge capacities of 708 and 617&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with 87% ICE, which is superior to previously reported anode materials as presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). In addition, at the initial 100 cycles, all electrodes show a slow capacity increase before stabilization, and the Mo-doped VS<sub>2</sub> performed an earlier capacity recovery. Combined with the charge-discharge curves of different cycles of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode, the increase of capacity comes from the enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion reaction and subsequent conversion reaction, which promotes the improvement of reversible capacity of the battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0025_R_d23124618e1484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater. Inorg. Chem. Front. Chem. Eng. J.">25</a>, <a href="#smll202308630-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0036_R_d23124618e1484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater. Small Small Energy Environ. Mater. Adv. Sci.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent cycles, the charge and discharge curves maintain a high degree of overlap, demonstrating smaller voltage polarization. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0003">3f</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) compares the electrochemical property of Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> with the rate performances of VS<sub>2</sub>-based anode reported and other sulfide-based anodes. When further tested at a higher, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0003">3g</a> shows the capacity retention of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> at a current density of 5A g<sup>−1</sup> as high as 80% up to 15&nbsp;000 cycles, and a capacity of 281 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 66.1% capacity retention after 20&nbsp;000 cycles.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical kinetics of the electrodes were studied using CV at various scan rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode showed two distinct reduction peaks and one distinct oxidation peak (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4a</a>). 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode showed stronger redox processes corresponding to three distinct reduction peaks and two distinct oxidation peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4d</a>). The pseudocapacitive behavior was quantitatively evaluated by using the Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The b-values of the oxidation peak and two reduction peaks for NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4b</a>) are calculated to be 0.86, −0.79, and −0.88, respectively. Compare with NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode, these three b-values of the reduction peaks and two oxidation peaks of the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4e</a>) are −0.95, −0.98, −0.81, 0.88, and 0.87, respectively. Both the b values of NVS<sub>2</sub> and 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrodes are closer to 1, representing high Na<sup>+</sup> pseudocapacitance storage behavior. Meanwhile, the contribution ratio of the diffusion control process and the pseudocapacitance control process can be calculated using Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The results show that the 5% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode has a higher pseudocapacitance contribution at different scan rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4c,f</a>). The 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode with high pseudocapacitance contribution exhibits superior reaction kinetics, higher specific capacity, and greater rate capability</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202308630-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/abcc916b-c9a3-483b-b2c9-d25176d5a05a/smll202308630-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/abcc916b-c9a3-483b-b2c9-d25176d5a05a/smll202308630-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9c97aca5-8772-48b8-aba1-fa14c9f730fe/smll202308630-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/abcc916b-c9a3-483b-b2c9-d25176d5a05a/smll202308630-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308630-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The CV curves at different scan rates for a) NVS<sub>2</sub>, and d) 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. The relationship between log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i> plots of the b) NVS<sub>2</sub>,and e) 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. The proportion of capacitive contributions for c) NVS<sub>2</sub>, and f) 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> at different scan rates. g) In situ EIS spectra of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> in the first cycle, h) Nyquist curves after 1000 cycles (inset: equivalent circuit mode corresponding to the Nyquist plots), i) the linear relationships between Z' and ω<sup>−1/2</sup>, j) the GITT curves of discharge and charge process, k) log of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients during the discharge–charge processes for NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>EIS and GITT measurements also revealed faster ion/electron transport in the 5% Mo-NVS2 electrode. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4g</a> shows the in situ EIS test results of 1st charge/discharge process of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode. The Nyquist plot mainly includes the electrolyte impedance (R<sub>s</sub>) in the high-frequency region, charge transfer impedance (R<sub>ct</sub>) in the mid-high-frequency region, and the Warburg impedance (Z<sub>w</sub>) related to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the low-frequency oblique line.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0037_R_d23124618e1627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The changes of R<sub>ct</sub> during different charge/discharge potentials is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). As the discharge goes from 2.1 to 1.5&nbsp;V, R<sub>ct</sub> gradually increases, indicating a decrease in charge transfer capability due to successive intercalation reactions during sodium embedding. However, when the discharge voltage is reduced from 1.5 to 0.3&nbsp;V, R<sub>ct</sub> gradually decreases, which may be due to the occurrence of a phase transition at a low potential, causing an enhancement of the charge transfer capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0038_R_d23124618e1642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano Chem. Eng. J.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The similarly impedance change occurs during the charging process at 1.5–1.8&nbsp;V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4h</a> further compares the EIS of NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub> electrodes after 1000 cycles. All electrodes have approximate R<sub>s</sub>, compared with the R<sub>ct</sub> (83.23 Ω) of NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode, the 3% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> 5% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, 10% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, and 20% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode have much smaller R<sub>ct</sub> (49.35, 33.80, 60.05, and 64.17 Ω) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), indicating after long-term charging and discharging, the Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub> electrodes still has superior charge transfer capability than NVS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>To further obtain the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion characteristics of different electrodes in the low frequency region, we fitted the Warburg coefficients with Equation&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). Apparently by the fitted results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4i</a>), The Warburg coefficient (<i>σ</i>) for NVS<sub>2</sub>, 3%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, 10%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, and 20%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> are 87.92, 32.31, 30.56, 58.45, and 63.03, respectively. According to Equation&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> is inversely proportional to <i>σ<sup>2</sup></i>. Consequently, 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> has the smallest <i>σ</i>, indicating the fastest ion diffusion rate.</p>
         
         <p>GITT was studied to to further characterize the Na + diffusion ability in the dynamic reaction process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4j</a>). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients at each potentials are approximated by Equation&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0004">4k</a> shows the relationship between the logarithm of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and potential. Comparing Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub> and NVS<sub>2</sub> electrodes, it is clearly observed that Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub> electrodes have higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients than NVS<sub>2</sub> electrodes at each potentials. Among them, the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode has the highest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (10<sup>−9</sup>-10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>), which is ≈4–5 orders of magnitude higher than that of the NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode, demonstrating the improved kinetics due to the generated sulfur vacancies and interlayer defect.</p>
         
         <p>A series of ex-situ characterizations were performed to study the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> anode. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5a,b</a> show the ex-situ XRD patterns of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode during the 1st discharge/charge process. The phase change of the electrode material in the contour plot is displayed by the color evolution. The XRD diffraction peaks representing the (001) and (002) planes of for VS<sub>2</sub> are clearly visible in 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> in the fresh state. When the discharge potential reaches to 1.8&nbsp;V, the peak intensity of (001) plane significantly weakens, along with another diffraction peak appears near 12.4°, indexing the (003) planes for Na<sub>0.5</sub>MoVS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS: 01-089-5280). When electrode further discharges to 0.6&nbsp;V, both the peak intensities of VS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.5</sub>MoVS<sub>2</sub> fade and disappear, followed by additional diffraction peaks near 13.4° and 34.3° appear, ascribing to (003) and (104) planes of NaMoVS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS:04-001-6162). It originates from the further Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation reaction in VS<sub>2</sub>. Besides, a small new diffraction peak can be observed at 38.9°. Continuing to discharge to 0.3&nbsp;V, the intensity of diffraction peak there gradually increases, and another new diffraction peak also appears at 42.2°. These two peaks can be indexed to the (220) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS:00-023-0441) and (110) plane of V metal (JCPDS:97-004-3420), respectively, indicating further conversion reaction between Na<sub>x</sub>MoVS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>. However, no diffraction peak representing Mo metal was observed at 0.3&nbsp;V. Subsequently, during charging from 0.3&nbsp;V to 2.4&nbsp;V, these peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S and V metal gradually disappear along with the reconstruction of the intercalation intermediate (Na<sub>x</sub>MoVS<sub>2</sub>). When continue charge to 3&nbsp;V, a broaden characteristic peak (9.2 <sup>o</sup>) of VS<sub>2</sub> reappear, indicating formation of VS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals. The peak position of VS<sub>2</sub> after cycle did not significantly shift, indicating that strong stability for NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalated in the VS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202308630-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/33061906-cf03-4c6a-ab45-cf9907565c31/smll202308630-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/33061906-cf03-4c6a-ab45-cf9907565c31/smll202308630-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/028c85ca-7dda-41af-a212-6488ee760b73/smll202308630-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/33061906-cf03-4c6a-ab45-cf9907565c31/smll202308630-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308630-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Discharging/charging curves and ex-situ XRD contour plots, b) corresponding ex-situ XRD patterns and c) ex-situ Raman spectra at different discharge-charge states. Ex-situ XPS spectrum variations of d) Na 1s, e) N 1s, f) Mo 3d, and g) V 2p at various electrochemical states during the initial cycle of discharge/charge. Fully discharged to 0.3&nbsp;V: h) HRTEM images and i) SAED images; Fully charged to 3.0&nbsp;V: j) HRTEM images and k) SAED images. The inset in Figure (h) and (j) shows a profile plot of the calibration for measuring the spacings.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5c</a> displays the ex-situ Raman spectra at various states. The characteristic peaks of the in-plane E<sub>1g</sub> (≈284 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and out-of-plane A<sub>1g</sub> (≈406 cm<sup>−1</sup>) vibration modes of VS<sub>2</sub> gradually diminish during the discharge from the initial state to 1.8&nbsp;V. It corresponds to the Na<sup>+</sup> inserted to VS<sub>2</sub> layers. However, when Na<sup>+</sup> continuously inserted between VS<sub>2</sub> layers as the voltage decreases, these characteristic peaks of E<sub>1g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> vibrational modes disappear. A new vibrational peak is observed at ≈451 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which can be attributed to the vibration of Na<sub>2</sub>S, further confirming the conversion reaction from Na<sub>x</sub>MoVS<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0039_R_d23124618e1886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the electrode recharged to 3.0&nbsp;V, the characteristic peak of the Na-S bond gradually disappeared and accompanied by the reappear of E<sub>1g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> vibrations of VS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>The valence state of the elements and their changes during the charge/discharge process were investigated by ex-situ XPS. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5d</a>, the intensity of the Na 1s peak at 1071.4&nbsp;eV binding energy in the fully discharged state is much higher than that in the fresh and fully charged states, indicating the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction in the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode. Obviously, a weak peak of Na compounds can be observed in the fully charged state, which could be due to the presence of Na compounds in the surface SEI film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0040_R_d23124618e1907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the high-resolution N 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5e</a>), it remains constant throughout the discharge-charge process, indicating that NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalated into the interlayer are not involved in the electrochemical reactions. As observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5f</a>, when the electrode discharge voltage reaches 0.3&nbsp;V, the S 2s binding energy is no longer observed, and the peak located at 228&nbsp;eV can be attributed to the metal Mo (Mo<sup>0</sup>) binding energy. Further it can be observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5g</a> that the V<sup>4+</sup> binding energy (523.6&nbsp;eV, 516.0&nbsp;eV) completely disappeared in the fully discharged state and V<sup>2+</sup> (520.9&nbsp;eV, 512.9&nbsp;eV) and V<sup>0</sup> (518.0&nbsp;eV, 512.3&nbsp;eV) binding energies are observed, indicating that the valence of V decreases with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. At the subsequent charge state, there is a strong V<sup>4+</sup> binding energy, which implies a reversible valence transition of V element.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5h</a> displays the HRTEM of 5% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode at fully discharge state. There are three lattice fringes in the image, which are 0.22, 0.21, and 0.33&nbsp;nm, respectively. The corresponding SAED pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5i</a>) from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5h</a> show three clear diffraction rings, confirming the existence of Mo and V and Na<sub>2</sub>S. In the fully charging state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5j</a>), it shows clear lattice fringes of 0.96 and 0.25&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (001) and (011) planes of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, the ring patterns of 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> phases are also observed in the SAED image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0005">5k</a>), confirming the highly reversible reaction and structural stability of the 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> electrode.</p>
         
         <p>To further reveal the fundamental mechanism of synergistic effects of Mo doping and NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> pre-intercalation, comprehensive DFT calculations were performed. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202308630-fig-0006">6a</a> shows the calculated total density of states (TDOS) and partial density of states (PDOS) for VS<sub>2</sub>, NVS<sub>2</sub> and 5% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. It shows band gap of ≈0.65&nbsp;eV and 0.5&nbsp;eV for VS<sub>2</sub> and NVS<sub>2</sub>, which has typical semiconductor properties. When the Mo<sup>4+</sup> is doped in the NVS<sub>2</sub> lattice, it exhibits a fully occupied state across the Fermi energy level, indicating its metallic nature. In addition, the conductivities of NVS<sub>2</sub>, 3% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, 5% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, 10% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>, and 20% Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> were tested by four-probe tester to be 16, 86, 96, 53, and 47 S&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, the electronic structure of VS<sub>2</sub> can be effectively regulated by NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> intercalation and Mo doping, which improve electron conductivity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202308630-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e2964e6a-fb9d-4856-97e9-2db280fb0805/smll202308630-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e2964e6a-fb9d-4856-97e9-2db280fb0805/smll202308630-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/325270fb-aaff-4965-89e9-4f437180ad74/smll202308630-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e2964e6a-fb9d-4856-97e9-2db280fb0805/smll202308630-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202308630-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202308630" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The calculated density of state (DOS) of VS<sub>2</sub>, NVS<sub>2</sub>, and 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub>. b) Calculated the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers for VS<sub>2</sub>, NVS<sub>2</sub>, 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> along the horizontal direction and 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> along the vertical direction. c) d-p orbital hybridization scenario among NaVS<sub>2</sub>, NaMoVS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S; d orbital spatial charge density for NaVS<sub>2</sub> and NaMoVS<sub>2</sub> are plotted on the right. d) Projected density of states on dz<sup>2</sup> and dxz/yz orbitals for NaVS<sub>2</sub> and NaMoVS<sub>2</sub>. e,f) Schematic representation of the d-p orbital hybridization state densities. The corresponding space charge density for each state is plotted on the right. A schematic of the orbital hybridization is given in the inset of the space charge density plot.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The diffusion energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> along different directions in the VS<sub>2</sub>, NVS<sub>2</sub> and Mo-doped NVS<sub>2</sub> structures are further calculated. When Na<sup>+</sup> migrates horizontally along the (001) crystal plane (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0006">6b</a>), the diffusion energy barriers for VS<sub>2</sub>, NVS<sub>2</sub> and 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> structures are 0.26, 0.06, and 0.03&nbsp;eV, respectively. The much lower diffusion energy barrier for 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> is responsible for the robust Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and migration capability during cycling. In addition, the cross-layer Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier through the (001) crystal plane for 5%Mo-NVS<sub>2</sub> is 0.15&nbsp;eV, which is much lower than the theoretical diffusion energy barrier for perfect VS<sub>2</sub> crystal structure (&gt;9&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202308630-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202308630-bib-0041_R_d23124618e2105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Eng. J.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result indicates that the interlayer defects constructed by Mo-doping opened up the cross-layer ion transport channel of VS<sub>2</sub>, which promoted the diffusion and migration ability of Na<sup>+</sup> from the perspective of nanostructure modulation.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202308630-fig-0006">6c</a> shows the effective electronic state in Na<sub>2</sub>S is dominated by the p orbitals of sulfur, and the electronic arrangement of the orbitals is divided into three states (px/y/z). The effective electronic states in NaVS<sub>2</sub> are dominated by the d orbitals of vanadium, showing four states: dz2, dxy, dxz/yz (quadratic simplicity), and dx2-y2, respectively. Based on the orbital symmetry, the d-p orbital hybridization between NaVS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S may form four electronic states σ, π, π*, and σ*.
